	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Common starling</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_Starling&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Starling">Common Starling</a>)</span></div>

</td></tr>












</tbody></table>
<p>The <b>common starling</b> (<i>Sturnus vulgaris</i>), also known as the <b>European starling</b> in the United States or simply the <b>starling</b> in the British Isles, is a medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a> <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> in the starling family, <a href="/wiki/Sturnidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Sturnidae">Sturnidae</a>. It is about 20&#160;cm (8&#160;in) long and has glossy black <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> with a metallic sheen, which is speckled with white at some times of year. The legs are pink and the bill is black in winter and yellow in summer; young birds have browner plumage than the adults. It is a noisy bird, especially in <a href="/wiki/Communal_roost" class="mw-redirect" title="Communal roost">communal roosts</a> and other gregarious situations, with an unmusical but varied song. Its gift for <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicry</a> has been noted in literature including the <i><a href="/wiki/Mabinogion" title="Mabinogion">Mabinogion</a></i> and the works of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>.
</p><p>The common starling has about 12 <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> breeding in open <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> across its native <a href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Range (biology)">range</a> in temperate Europe and across the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a> to western Mongolia, and it has been introduced to <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> This bird is resident in western and southern Europe and southwestern Asia, while northeastern populations <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrate</a> south and west in the winter within the breeding range and also further south to <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a> and North Africa. The common starling builds an untidy <a href="/wiki/Nest" title="Nest">nest</a> in a natural or artificial cavity in which four or five glossy, pale blue eggs are laid. These take two weeks to hatch and the young remain in the nest for another three weeks. There are normally one or two breeding attempts each year. This species is omnivorous, taking a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, as well as seeds and fruit. It is hunted by various mammals and <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>, and is host to a range of external and internal parasites.
</p><p>Large flocks typical of this species can be beneficial to agriculture by controlling invertebrate pests; however, starlings can also be pests themselves when they feed on fruit and sprouting crops. Common starlings may also be a nuisance through the noise and mess caused by their large urban roosts. Introduced populations in particular have been subjected to a range of controls, including <a href="/wiki/Culling" title="Culling">culling</a>, but these have had limited success, except in preventing the colonisation of Western Australia.
</p><p>The species has declined in numbers in parts of northern and western Europe since the 1980s due to fewer grassland invertebrates being available as food for growing chicks. Despite this, its huge global population is not thought to be declining significantly, so the common starling is classified as being of <a href="/wiki/Least_Concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least Concern">Least Concern</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_systematics">Taxonomy and systematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and systematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The common starling was first described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i> in 1758 under its current binomial name.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <i>Sturnus</i> and <i>vulgaris</i> are derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "starling" and "common" respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-jobling302_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jobling302-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The Old English <i>staer</i>, later <i>stare</i>, and the Latin <i>sturnus</i> are both derived from an unknown <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Indo-European</a> root dating back to the second millennium BC. "Starling" was first recorded in the 11th century, when it referred to the juvenile of the species, but by the 16th century it had already largely supplanted "stare" to refer to birds of all ages.<sup id="cite_ref-lockwood_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lockwood-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The older name is referenced in <a href="/wiki/W._B._Yeats" title="W. B. Yeats">William Butler Yeats'</a> poem "The Stare's Nest by My Window".<sup id="cite_ref-wbyeats_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wbyeats-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Ornithological_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="International Ornithological Congress">International Ornithological Congress</a>' preferred English vernacular name is common starling.<sup id="cite_ref-gill_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gill-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Starling" title="Starling">starling</a> family, Sturnidae, is an entirely <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> group apart from introductions elsewhere, with the greatest numbers of species in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-feare13_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare13-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The genus <i>Sturnus</i> is <a href="/wiki/Polyphyly" title="Polyphyly">polyphyletic</a> and relationships between its members are not fully resolved. The closest relation of the common starling is the <a href="/wiki/Spotless_starling" title="Spotless starling">spotless starling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-zuccon2006_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zuccon2006-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The non-migratory spotless starling may be descended from a population of ancestral <i>S. vulgaris</i> that survived in an <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian</a> <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum_refugia" title="Last Glacial Maximum refugia">refugium</a> during an <a href="/wiki/Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a> retreat,<sup id="cite_ref-zuccon2008_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zuccon2008-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondria" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitochondria">mitochondrial</a> <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> studies suggest that it could be considered a subspecies of the common starling. There is more genetic variation between common starling populations than between the nominate common starling and the spotless starling.<sup id="cite_ref-neves_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neves-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Although common starling remains are known from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Pleistocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Pleistocene">Middle Pleistocene</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bedetti_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bedetti-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> part of the problem in resolving relationships in the Sturnidae is the paucity of the fossil record for the family as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-zuccon2008_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zuccon2008-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are several <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of the common starling, which vary <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinally</a> in size and the colour tone of the adult plumage. The gradual variation over geographic range and extensive <a href="/wiki/Intergradation" title="Intergradation">intergradation</a> means that acceptance of the various subspecies varies between authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpc_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpc-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vaurie_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vaurie-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed center">

</caption>

<th scope="col" width="10%">Subspecies
</th>
<th scope="col" width="8%">Authority
</th>
<th scope="col" width="22%">Range
</th>
<th scope="col" width="38%">Comments
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">European starling<br />(<i>S. v. vulgaris</i>)
</th>
<td>Linnaeus, 1758
</td>
<td>Most of Europe, except the far northwest and far southeast; also <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>
</td>
<td>The <a href="/wiki/Nominate_subspecies" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominate subspecies">nominate subspecies</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Faroe starling<br />(<i>S. v. faroensis</i>)
</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Henry_Wemyss_Feilden" title="Henry Wemyss Feilden">Feilden</a>, 1872
</td>
<td>the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>
</td>
<td>Slightly larger than nominate, especially in the bill and feet. Adult with darker and duller green gloss and far less spotting, even in fresh plumage. Juvenile sooty black with whitish chin and areas on the belly; the throat spotted black.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Shetland starling<br />(<i>S. v. zetlandicus</i>)
</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ernst_Hartert" title="Ernst Hartert">Hartert</a>, 1918.
</td>
<td>the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Islands">Shetland Islands</a>
</td>
<td>Like <i>faroensis</i>, but intermediate in size between that subspecies and <i>vulgaris</i>. Birds from <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle" title="Fair Isle">Fair Isle</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Scotland" title="St Kilda, Scotland">St. Kilda</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a> are intermediate between this subspecies and the nominate and placement with <i>vulgaris</i> or <i>zetlandicus</i> varies according to the authority.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Azores starling<br />(<i>S. v. granti</i>)
</th>
<td>Hartert, 1903
</td>
<td>the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>
</td>
<td>Like the nominate, but smaller, especially the feet. Often strong purple gloss on the upperparts.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Siberian starling<br />(<i>S. v. poltaratskyi</i>)
</th>
<td>(<a href="/wiki/Otto_Finsch" title="Otto Finsch">Finsch</a>, 1878)
</td>
<td>Eastern <a href="/wiki/Bashkortostan" title="Bashkortostan">Bashkortostan</a> eastwards through the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a> and central <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baykal" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Baykal">Lake Baykal</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>
</td>
<td>Like the nominate, but gloss on the head predominantly purple, on the back green, on the flanks usually purplish-blue, on the upper wing-coverts bluish-green. In flight, conspicuous light cinnamon-buff fringes to the under wing-coverts and axillaries; these areas may appear very pale in fresh plumage.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Black Sea starling<br />(<i>S. v. tauricus</i>)
</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Sergei_Aleksandrovich_Buturlin" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergei Aleksandrovich Buturlin">Buturlin</a>, 1904
</td>
<td>From <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> and east of the <a href="/wiki/Dnieper_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Dnieper River">Dnieper River</a> eastwards around the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> to western <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. Not found in the uplands, where it is replaced by <i>purpurascens</i>.
</td>
<td>Like the nominate, but decidedly long-winged. Gloss of the head green, of the body bronze-purple, of the flanks and upper wing-coverts greenish-bronze. The underwings blackish with pale fringes of the coverts. Nearly spotless in breeding plumage.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Eastern Turkey starling<br />(<i>S. v. purpurascens</i>)
</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/John_Gould" title="John Gould">Gould</a>, 1868
</td>
<td>Eastern Turkey to <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Sevan" title="Lake Sevan">Lake Sevan</a>, in the uplands on the eastern shore of the Black Sea, replacing <i>tauricus</i>
</td>
<td>Like the nominate, but the wings longer and green gloss restricted to the ear-coverts, neck and upper chest. Purple gloss elsewhere except on the flanks and upper wing-coverts, where it is more bronzy. Dark underwings with slim white fringes to the coverts.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Caucasian starling<br />(<i>S. v. caucasicus</i>)
</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Theodore_K_Lorenz" class="mw-redirect" title="Theodore K Lorenz">Lorenz</a>, 1887
</td>
<td>the <a href="/wiki/Volga_Delta" title="Volga Delta">Volga Delta</a> through the eastern <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and adjacent areas
</td>
<td>Green gloss on the head and back, purple gloss on the neck and belly, more bluish on the upper wing-coverts. The underwings like <i>purpurascens</i>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Central Asian starling<br />(<i>S. v. porphyronotus</i>)
</th>
<td>(<a href="/wiki/Richard_Bowdler_Sharpe" title="Richard Bowdler Sharpe">Sharpe</a>, 1888)
</td>
<td>Western <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, grading into <i>poltaratskyi</i> between the <a href="/wiki/Dzungarian_Alatau" title="Dzungarian Alatau">Dzungarian Alatau</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a>
</td>
<td>Very similar to <i>tauricus</i>, but smaller and completely <a href="/wiki/Allopatric" class="mw-redirect" title="Allopatric">allopatric</a>, being separated by <i>purpurascens</i>, <i>caucasicus</i> and <i>nobilior</i>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Hume's starling<br />or Afghan starling<br />(<i>S. v. nobilior</i>)
</th>
<td>(<a href="/wiki/Allan_Octavian_Hume" title="Allan Octavian Hume">Hume</a>, 1879)
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, southeastern <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and adjacent <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> to eastern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>
</td>
<td>Like <i>purpurascens</i>, but smaller and the wings shorter; the ear-coverts glossed purple, and the underside and upperwing gloss quite reddish.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Himalayan starling<br />(<i>S. v. humii</i>)
</th>
<td>(<a href="/wiki/William_Edwin_Brooks" title="William Edwin Brooks">Brooks</a>, 1876)
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>
</td>
<td>Small; purple gloss restricted to the neck area and sometimes the flanks to the tail-coverts, otherwise glossed green. This is sometimes treated under the name <i>indicus</i> given by <a href="/wiki/Brian_Houghton_Hodgson" title="Brian Houghton Hodgson">Hodgson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Sindh starling<br />(<i>S. v. minor</i>)
</th>
<td>(Hume, 1873)
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>
</td>
<td>Small; green gloss restricted to the head and lower belly and back, otherwise glossed purple.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Birds from <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle" title="Fair Isle">Fair Isle</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Scotland" title="St Kilda, Scotland">St. Kilda</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a> are intermediate in size between <i>S. v. zetlandicus</i> and the nominate form, and their subspecies placement varies according to the authority. The dark juveniles typical of these island forms are occasionally found in mainland Scotland and elsewhere, indicating some <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> from <i>faroensis</i> or <i>zetlandicus</i>, subspecies formerly considered to be isolated.<sup id="cite_ref-nevesglas_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nevesglas-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-parkin_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parkin-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several other subspecies have been named, but are generally no longer considered valid. Most are intergrades that occur where the ranges of various subspecies meet. These include: <i>S. v. ruthenus</i> <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Aleksandrovich_Menzbier" class="mw-redirect" title="Mikhail Aleksandrovich Menzbier">Menzbier</a>, 1891 and <i>S. v. jitkowi</i> Buturlin, 1904, which are intergrades between <i>vulgaris</i> and <i>poltaratskyi</i> from western Russia; <i>S. v. graecus</i> <a href="/wiki/Viktor_von_Tschusi_zu_Schmidhoffen" title="Viktor von Tschusi zu Schmidhoffen">Tschusi</a>, 1905 and <i>S. v. balcanicus</i> Buturlin and Harms, 1909, which are intergrades between <i>vulgaris</i> and <i>tauricus</i> from the southern <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> to central <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and throughout Greece to the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a>; and <i>S. v. heinrichi</i> <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Stresemann" title="Erwin Stresemann">Stresemann</a>, 1928, an intergrade between <i>caucasicus</i> and <i>nobilior</i> in northern Iran. <i>S. v. persepolis</i> <a href="/wiki/Claud_Buchanan_Ticehurst" class="mw-redirect" title="Claud Buchanan Ticehurst">Ticehurst</a>, 1928 from southern Iran's (<a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Fars Province</a>) is very similar to <i>vulgaris</i>, and it is not clear whether it is a distinct resident population or simply migrants from southeastern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-vaurie_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vaurie-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens,_London,_England_-juvenile-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Juvenile" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens%2C_London%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg/220px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens%2C_London%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens%2C_London%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg/330px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens%2C_London%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens%2C_London%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg/440px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens%2C_London%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2424" data-file-height="1619" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-Kensington_Gardens,_London,_England_-juvenile-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young juvenile perching on a table in London. Its plumage is mainly grey-brown</div></div></div>
<p>The common starling is 19–23&#160;cm (7.5–9.1&#160;in) long, with a wingspan of 31–44&#160;cm (12–17&#160;in) and a weight of 58–101&#160;g (2.0–3.6&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Among standard measurements, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> is 11.8 to 13.8&#160;cm (4.6 to 5.4&#160;in), the <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> is 5.8 to 6.8&#160;cm (2.3 to 2.7&#160;in), the <a href="/wiki/Culmen_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Culmen (bird)">culmen</a> is 2.5 to 3.2&#160;cm (0.98 to 1.26&#160;in) and the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> is 2.7 to 3.2&#160;cm (1.1 to 1.3&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> is <a href="/wiki/Iridescence" title="Iridescence">iridescent</a> black, glossed purple or green, and spangled with white, especially in winter. The underparts of adult male common starlings are less spotted than those of adult females at a given time of year. The throat feathers of males are long and loose and are used in display while those of females are smaller and more pointed. The legs are stout and pinkish- or greyish-red. The bill is narrow and conical with a sharp tip; in the winter it is brownish-black but in summer, females have lemon yellow beaks while males have yellow bills with blue-grey bases. <a href="/wiki/Moult" class="mw-redirect" title="Moult">Moulting</a> occurs once a year- in late summer after the breeding season has finished; the fresh feathers are prominently tipped white (breast feathers) or buff (wing and back feathers), which gives the bird a speckled appearance. The reduction in the spotting in the breeding season is achieved through the white feather tips largely wearing off. Juveniles are grey-brown and by their first winter resemble adults though often retaining some brown juvenile feathering, especially on the head.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpc_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpc-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Coward_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> They can usually be sexed by the colour of the irises, rich brown in males, mouse-brown or grey in females. Estimating the contrast between an <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">iris</a> and the central always-dark <a href="/wiki/Pupil" title="Pupil">pupil</a> is 97% accurate in determining sex, rising to 98% if the length of the throat feathers is also considered.<sup id="cite_ref-smithcuthill_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithcuthill-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harrison1928_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrison1928-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The common starling is mid-sized by both starling standards and passerine standards. It is readily distinguished from other mid-sized passerines, such as <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icterid" title="Icterid">icterids</a> or small <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, by its relatively short tail, sharp, blade-like bill, round-bellied shape and strong, sizeable (and rufous-coloured) legs. In flight, its strongly pointed wings and dark colouration are distinctive, while on the ground its strange, somewhat waddling gait is also characteristic. The colouring and build usually distinguish this bird from other starlings, although the closely related <a href="/wiki/Spotless_starling" title="Spotless starling">spotless starling</a> may be physically distinguished by the lack of iridescent spots in adult breeding plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Juvenile moulting" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg/220px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg/330px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg/440px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3173" data-file-height="2292" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-California-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An immature in California. It has partly moulted into its first-winter plumage; however, juvenile brown plumage is prominent on its head and neck</div></div></div>
<p>Like most terrestrial starlings the common starling moves by walking or running, rather than hopping. Their flight is quite strong and direct; their triangular-shaped wings beat very rapidly, and periodically the birds glide for a short way without losing much height before resuming powered flight. When in a flock, the birds take off almost simultaneously, wheel and turn in unison, form a compact mass or trail off into a wispy stream, bunch up again and land in a coordinated fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Common starling on migration can fly at 60–80&#160;km/h (37–50&#160;mph) and cover up to 1,000–1,500&#160;km (620–930&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-linz_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linz-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several terrestrial starlings, including those in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Sturnus" title="Sturnus">Sturnus</a></i>, have adaptations of the skull and muscles that help with feeding by probing.<sup id="cite_ref-feare21_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare21-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> This adaptation is most strongly developed in the common starling (along with the <a href="/wiki/Sturnus_unicolor" class="mw-redirect" title="Sturnus unicolor">spotless</a> and <a href="/wiki/White-cheeked_starling" title="White-cheeked starling">white-cheeked starlings</a>), where the <a href="/wiki/Muscle_protractor_pterygoidei_et_quadrati" title="Muscle protractor pterygoidei et quadrati">protractor muscles</a> responsible for opening the jaw are enlarged and the skull is narrow, allowing the eye to be moved forward to peer down the length of the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw2_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw2-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> This technique involves inserting the bill into the ground and opening it as a way of searching for hidden food items. Common starlings have the physical traits that enable them to use this feeding technique, which has undoubtedly helped the species spread far and wide.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Iberia, the western Mediterranean and northwest Africa, the common starling may be confused with the closely related spotless starling, the plumage of which, as its name implies, has a more uniform colour. At close range it can be seen that the latter has longer throat feathers, a fact particularly noticeable when it sings.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalization">Vocalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vocalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="40.294943310658" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Common_Starling_(Sturnus_vulgaris)_(W1CDR0001431_BD8).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e5/Common_Starling_%28Sturnus_vulgaris%29_%28W1CDR0001431_BD8%29.ogg/Common_Starling_%28Sturnus_vulgaris%29_%28W1CDR0001431_BD8%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="135584" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Common_Starling_%28Sturnus_vulgaris%29_%28W1CDR0001431_BD8%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (200 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="199752" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Starling_(Sturnus_vulgaris)_(W1CDR0001431_BD8).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chattering calls of a group</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birdsinging03182006.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Singing adult male" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Birdsinging03182006.JPG/220px-Birdsinging03182006.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Birdsinging03182006.JPG/330px-Birdsinging03182006.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Birdsinging03182006.JPG/440px-Birdsinging03182006.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1988" data-file-height="1616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birdsinging03182006.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult male singing and displaying its long throat feathers</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg/220px-Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg/330px-Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg/440px-Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1888" data-file-height="1048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spinus-common-starling-2015-03-n032330-w.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Singing <i>Sturnus vulgaris</i>, <a href="/wiki/Spring_Creek_Park" title="Spring Creek Park">Spring Creek Park</a>, New York, USA.</div></div></div>
<p>The common starling is a noisy bird. Its <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Bird vocalisation">song</a> consists of a wide variety of both melodic and mechanical-sounding noises as part of a ritual succession of sounds. The male is the main songster and engages in bouts of song lasting for a minute or more. Each of these typically includes four varieties of song type, which follow each other in a regular order without pause. The bout starts with a series of pure-tone whistles and these are followed by the main part of the song, a number of variable sequences that often incorporate snatches of song <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicked</a> from other species of bird and various naturally occurring or man-made noises. The structure and simplicity of the sound mimicked is of greater importance than the frequency with which it occurs. In some instances, a wild starling has been observed to mimic a sound it has heard only once. Each sound clip is repeated several times before the bird moves on to the next. After this variable section comes a number of types of repeated clicks followed by a final burst of high-frequency song, again formed of several types. Each bird has its own <a href="/wiki/Musical_repertoire" title="Musical repertoire">repertoire</a> with more proficient birds having a range of up to 35 variable song types and as many as 14 types of clicks.<sup id="cite_ref-Feare54_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feare54-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Males sing constantly as the breeding period approaches and perform less often once pairs have bonded. In the presence of a female, a male sometimes flies to his nest and sings from the entrance, apparently attempting to entice the female in. Older birds tend to have a wider repertoire than younger ones. Those males that engage in longer bouts of singing and that have wider repertoires attract mates earlier and have greater <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_success" title="Reproductive success">reproductive success</a> than others. Females appear to prefer mates with more complex songs, perhaps because this indicates greater experience or longevity. Having a complex song is also useful in defending a territory and deterring less experienced males from encroaching.<sup id="cite_ref-Feare54_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feare54-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Singing also occurs outside the breeding season, taking place throughout the year apart from the moulting period. The songsters are more commonly male although females also sing on occasion. The function of such out-of-season song is poorly understood.<sup id="cite_ref-Feare54_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feare54-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Eleven other types of call have been described including a flock call, threat call, attack call, snarl call and copulation call.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The alarm call is a harsh scream, and while foraging together common starlings squabble incessantly.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> They chatter while roosting and bathing, making a great deal of noise that can cause irritation to people living nearby. When a flock of common starlings is flying together, the synchronised movements of the birds' wings make a distinctive whooshing sound that can be heard hundreds of metres away.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour_and_ecology">Behaviour and ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Behaviour and ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>The common starling is a highly gregarious species, especially in autumn and winter. Although <a href="/wiki/Group_size_measures" title="Group size measures">flock size</a> is highly variable, huge, noisy flocks - murmurations - may form near roosts. These dense concentrations of birds are thought to be a defence against attacks by <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Flocks form a tight <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a>-like formation in flight, frequently expanding and contracting and changing shape, seemingly without any sort of leader. Each common starling changes its course and speed as a result of the movement of its closest neighbours.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Very large roosts, up to 1.5&#160;million birds, form in city centres, woodlands and reedbeds, causing problems with their droppings. These may accumulate up to 30&#160;cm (12&#160;in) deep, killing trees by their concentration of chemicals. In smaller amounts, the droppings act as a <a href="/wiki/Fertiliser" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertiliser">fertiliser</a>, and therefore woodland managers may try to move roosts from one area of a wood to another to benefit from the soil enhancement and avoid large toxic deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-currie_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-currie-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Flocks of more than a million common starlings may be observed just before sunset in spring in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">Jutland</a>, Denmark over the seaward <a href="/wiki/Marshland" class="mw-redirect" title="Marshland">marshlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B8nder" title="Tønder">Tønder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Esbjerg" title="Esbjerg">Esbjerg</a> municipalities between Tønder and <a href="/wiki/Ribe" title="Ribe">Ribe</a>. They gather in March until northern Scandinavian birds leave for their breeding ranges by mid-April. Their <a href="/wiki/Swarm_behaviour" title="Swarm behaviour">swarm behaviour</a> creates complex shapes silhouetted against the sky, a phenomenon known locally as <i><a href="/wiki/Sort_sol_(bird_flock)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sort sol (bird flock)">sort sol</a></i> ("black sun").<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
Flocks of anything from five to fifty thousand common starlings form in areas of the UK just before sundown during mid-winter. These flocks are commonly called murmurations.<sup id="cite_ref-West_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg/220px-A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg/330px-A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg/440px-A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_field_at_Aghagallon_-_geograph.org.uk_-_488583.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A flock foraging at a farm in Northern Ireland</div></div></div>
<p>The common starling is largely <a href="/wiki/Insectivorous" class="mw-redirect" title="Insectivorous">insectivorous</a> and feeds on both pest and other <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>. The food range includes <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crane_fly" title="Crane fly">crane flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayfly" title="Mayfly">mayflies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damsel_fly" class="mw-redirect" title="Damsel fly">damsel flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earwig" title="Earwig">earwigs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lacewing" class="mw-redirect" title="Lacewing">lacewings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caddisfly" title="Caddisfly">caddisflies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sawfly" title="Sawfly">sawflies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>. Prey are consumed in both adult and larvae stages of development, and common starlings will also feed on <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a>, small <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Adeney_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adeney-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> While the consumption of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Invertebrates">invertebrates</a> is necessary for successful breeding, common starlings are <a href="/wiki/Omnivorous" class="mw-redirect" title="Omnivorous">omnivorous</a> and can also eat <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seeds" class="mw-redirect" title="Seeds">seeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruits" class="mw-redirect" title="Fruits">fruits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_waste" title="Food waste">food waste</a> if the opportunity arises.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-emu_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emu-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pestnote_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pestnote-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The Sturnidae differ from most birds in that they cannot easily metabolise foods containing high levels of <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, although they can cope with other fruits such as grapes and cherries.<sup id="cite_ref-bvaawf_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bvaawf-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The isolated Azores subspecies of the common starling eats the eggs of the endangered <a href="/wiki/Roseate_tern" title="Roseate tern">roseate tern</a>. Measures are being introduced to reduce common starling populations by culling before the terns return to their breeding colonies in spring.<sup id="cite_ref-neves_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neves-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_4" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:720px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_5&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;727280&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97002997003&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Feeding the young starlings.ogv" style="width:220px;height:147px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Feeding_the_young_starlings.ogv/220px-seek%3D40-Feeding_the_young_starlings.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Feeding_the_young_starlings.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Feeding_the_young_starlings.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult foraging and finding food for young chicks</div></div></div>
<p>There are several methods by which common starlings obtain their food but for the most part, they forage close to the ground, taking insects from the surface or just underneath. Generally, common starlings prefer foraging amongst short-cropped grasses and eat with grazing animals or perch on their backs,<sup id="cite_ref-pestnote_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pestnote-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> where they will also feed on the mammal's external parasites.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Large flocks may engage in a practice known as "roller-feeding", where the birds at the back of the flock continually fly to the front where the feeding opportunities are best.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The larger the flock, the nearer individuals are to one another while foraging. Flocks often feed in one place for some time, and return to previous successfully foraged sites.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are three types of foraging behaviour observed in the common starling. "Probing" involves the bird plunging its beak into the ground randomly and repetitively until an insect has been found, and is often accompanied by bill gaping where the bird opens its beak in the soil to enlarge a hole. This behaviour, first described by <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Lorenz" title="Konrad Lorenz">Konrad Lorenz</a> and given the German term <i>zirkeln</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> is also used to create and widen holes in plastic garbage bags. It takes time for young common starlings to perfect this technique, and because of this the diet of young birds will often contain fewer insects.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Hawking_(birds)" title="Hawking (birds)">Hawking</a>" is the capture of flying insects directly from the air, and "lunging" is the less common technique of striking forward to catch a moving <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> on the ground. Earthworms are caught by pulling from soil.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Common starlings that have periods without access to food, or have a reduction in the hours of light available for feeding, compensate by increasing their body mass by the deposition of fat.<sup id="cite_ref-witter_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-witter-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting">Nesting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nesting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea,_Essex,_England_-nest-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea%2C_Essex%2C_England_-nest-8.jpg/170px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea%2C_Essex%2C_England_-nest-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea%2C_Essex%2C_England_-nest-8.jpg/255px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea%2C_Essex%2C_England_-nest-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea%2C_Essex%2C_England_-nest-8.jpg/340px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea%2C_Essex%2C_England_-nest-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="987" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-Southend-on-Sea,_Essex,_England_-nest-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A parent feeding a chick in a nest in a tree hole in England</div></div></div>
<p>Unpaired males find a suitable cavity and begin to build nests in order to attract single females, often decorating the nest with ornaments such as flowers and fresh green material, which the female later disassembles upon accepting him as a mate.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brouwer_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brouwer-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The amount of green material is not important, as long as some is present, but the presence of <a href="/wiki/Herbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbs">herbs</a> in the decorative material appears to be significant in attracting a mate. The scent of plants such as <a href="/wiki/Achillea_millefolium" title="Achillea millefolium">yarrow</a> acts as an <a href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">olfactory</a> attractant to females.<sup id="cite_ref-Brouwer_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brouwer-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gwinner_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gwinner-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The males sing throughout much of the construction and even more so when a female approaches his nest. Following <a href="/wiki/Animal_sexual_behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal sexual behavior">copulation</a>, the male and female continue to build the nest. Nests may be in any type of hole, common locations include inside hollowed trees, buildings, tree stumps and man-made nest-boxes.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <i>S. v. zetlandicus</i> typically breeds in crevices and holes in cliffs, a habitat only rarely used by the nominate form.<sup id="cite_ref-michael_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michael-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Nests are typically made out of straw, dry grass and twigs with an inner lining made up of feathers, wool and soft leaves. Construction usually takes four or five days and may continue through incubation.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common starlings are both <a href="/wiki/Monogamous" class="mw-redirect" title="Monogamous">monogamous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polygamous" class="mw-redirect" title="Polygamous">polygamous</a>; although broods are generally brought up by one male and one female, occasionally the pair may have an extra helper. Pairs may be part of a colony, in which case several other nests may occupy the same or nearby trees.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Males may mate with a second female while the first is still on the nest. The reproductive success of the bird is poorer in the second nest than it is in the primary nest and is better when the male remains monogamous.<sup id="cite_ref-sandell_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandell-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starling_eggs.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Starling_eggs.jpeg/220px-Starling_eggs.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Starling_eggs.jpeg/330px-Starling_eggs.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Starling_eggs.jpeg/440px-Starling_eggs.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2608" data-file-height="1952" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starling_eggs.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Five eggs in a nest</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG/220px-Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG/330px-Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG/440px-Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3760" data-file-height="2760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_MWNH_2328.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eggs, Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a>, Germany</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Chicks waiting to be fed" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg/220px-Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg/330px-Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg/440px-Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3672" data-file-height="2565" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starling_chicks_Clarinbridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chicks waiting to be fed at the entrance of their nest made in a gap in a wall in <a href="/wiki/Galway" title="Galway">Galway</a>, Ireland</div></div></div>
<p>Breeding takes place during the spring and summer. Following copulation, the female lays eggs on a daily basis over a period of several days. If an egg is lost during this time, she will lay another to replace it. There are normally four or five eggs that are <a href="/wiki/Ovoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ovoid">ovoid</a> in shape and pale blue or occasionally white, and they commonly have a glossy appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The colour of the eggs seems to have evolved through the relatively good visibility of blue at low light levels.<sup id="cite_ref-weg_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weg-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The egg size is 26.5–34.5&#160;mm (1.04–1.36&#160;in) in length and 20.0–22.5&#160;mm (0.79–0.89&#160;in) in maximum diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian incubation">Incubation</a> lasts thirteen days, although the last egg laid may take 24&#160;hours longer than the first to hatch. Both parents share the responsibility of brooding the eggs, but the female spends more time incubating them than does the male, and is the only parent to do so at night when the male returns to the communal roost. The young are born blind and naked. They develop light fluffy down within seven days of hatching and can see within nine days.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Once the chicks are able to regulate their body temperature, about six days after hatching,<sup id="cite_ref-oulu_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oulu-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> the adults largely cease removing droppings from the nest. Prior to that, the fouling would wet both the chicks' plumage and the nest material, thereby reducing their effectiveness as insulation and increasing the risk of chilling the hatchlings.<sup id="cite_ref-burton_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burton-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nestlings" class="mw-redirect" title="Nestlings">Nestlings</a> remain in the nest for three weeks, where they are fed continuously by both parents. <a href="/wiki/Fledgling_(birds)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fledgling (birds)">Fledglings</a> continue to be fed by their parents for another one or two weeks. A pair can raise up to three broods per year, frequently reusing and relining the same nest,<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> although two broods is typical,<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> or just one north of 48°N.<sup id="cite_ref-linz_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linz-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Within two months, most juveniles will have moulted and gained their first basic plumage. They acquire their adult plumage the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> As with other passerines, the nest is kept clean and the chicks' <a href="/wiki/Fecal_sac" title="Fecal sac">faecal sacs</a> are removed by the adults.<sup id="cite_ref-wright_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wright-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Intraspecific_hybrid" class="mw-redirect" title="Intraspecific hybrid">Intraspecific</a> <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasites" class="mw-redirect" title="Brood parasites">brood parasites</a> are common in common starling nests. Female "floaters" (unpaired females during the breeding season) present in colonies often lay eggs in another pair's nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandell_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandell-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Fledglings have also been reported to invade their own or neighbouring nests and evict a new brood.<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Common starling nests have a 48% to 79% rate of successful fledging, although only 20% of nestlings survive to breeding age; the adult survival rate is closer to 60%. The average life span is about 2–3 years,<sup id="cite_ref-linz_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linz-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> with a longevity record of 22&#160;yr 11&#160;m.<sup id="cite_ref-euring_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euring-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators_and_parasites">Predators and parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Predators and parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A majority of starling predators are avian. The typical response of starling groups is to take flight, with a common sight being undulating flocks of starling flying high in quick and agile patterns. Their abilities in flight are seldom matched by birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Adult common starlings are hunted by <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gentilis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-gensbolhawk_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gensbolhawk-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a> including the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_hobby" title="Eurasian hobby">Eurasian hobby</a> (<i>Falco subbuteo</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> (<i>Falco tinnunculus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-gensbolfalcon_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gensbolfalcon-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Slower raptors like <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kites</a> (<i>Milvus migrans &amp; milvus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Swamp_harrier" title="Swamp harrier">Australasian harrier</a> (<i>Circus approximans</i>) tend to take the more easily caught fledglings or juveniles.<sup id="cite_ref-gensbolkite_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gensbolkite-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-baker_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baker-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> While perched in groups by night, they can be vulnerable to owls, including the <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a> (<i>Athene noctua</i>), <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>), <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a> (<i>Strix aluco</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More than twenty species of <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcon</a> are known to occasionally predate feral starlings in North America, though the most regular predators of adults are likely to be urban-living <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlins</a> (<i>Falco columbarius</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Common_myna" title="Common myna">Common mynas</a> (<i>Acridotheres tristis</i>) sometimes evict eggs, nestlings and adult common starlings from their nests,<sup id="cite_ref-H1923_31-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1923-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_honeyguide" title="Lesser honeyguide">lesser honeyguide</a> (<i>Indicator minor</i>), a <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasite</a>, uses the common starling as a host.<sup id="cite_ref-iziko_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iziko-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Starlings are more commonly the culprits rather than victims of nest eviction however, especially towards other starlings and <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">woodpeckers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Nests can be raided by mammals capable of climbing to them, such as <a href="/wiki/Stoat" title="Stoat">stoats</a> (<i>Mustela erminea</i>), <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a> (<i>Procyon lotor</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-bull_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bull-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a> (<i>Sciurus</i> spp.),<sup id="cite_ref-linz_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linz-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and cats may catch the unwary.<sup id="cite_ref-cats_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cats-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common starlings are hosts to a wide range of parasites. A survey of three hundred common starlings from six US states found that all had at least one type of parasite; 99% had external fleas, mites or ticks, and 95% carried internal parasites, mostly various types of worm. <a href="/wiki/Blood-sucking" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood-sucking">Blood-sucking</a> species leave their host when it dies, but other external parasites stay on the corpse. A bird with a deformed bill was heavily infested with <i><a href="/wiki/Mallophaga" title="Mallophaga">Mallophaga</a></i> lice, presumably due to its inability to remove vermin.<sup id="cite_ref-boyd_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boyd-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Parasitic mite" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg/220px-Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg/330px-Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg/440px-Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dermanyssus_gallinae_mite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Dermanyssus_gallinae" title="Dermanyssus gallinae">Dermanyssus gallinae</a></i>, a parasite of the common starling</div></div></div>
<p>The hen flea (<i><a href="/wiki/Ceratophyllus_gallinae" title="Ceratophyllus gallinae">Ceratophyllus gallinae</a></i>) is the most common <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">flea</a> in their nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild84_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild84-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The small, pale house-sparrow flea <i>C. fringillae</i>, is also occasionally found there and probably arises from the habit of its main host of taking over the nests of other species. This flea does not occur in the US, even on <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild115_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild115-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louse" title="Louse">Lice</a> include <i>Menacanthus eurystemus</i>, <i>Brueelia nebulosa</i> and <i>Stumidoecus sturni</i>. Other arthropod parasites include <i><a href="/wiki/Ixodes" title="Ixodes">Ixodes</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a> such as <i>Analgopsis passerinus</i>, <i>Boydaia stumi</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dermanyssus_gallinae" title="Dermanyssus gallinae">Dermanyssus gallinae</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ornithonyssus_bursa" title="Ornithonyssus bursa">Ornithonyssus bursa</a></i>, <i>O. sylviarum</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Proctophyllodes" title="Proctophyllodes">Proctophyllodes</a></i> species, <i>Pteronyssoides truncatus</i> and <i>Trouessartia rosteri</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-H1960_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1960-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The hen mite <i>D. gallinae</i> is itself preyed upon by the predatory mite <i><a href="/wiki/Androlaelaps_casalis" title="Androlaelaps casalis">Androlaelaps casalis</a></i>. The presence of this control on numbers of the parasitic species may explain why birds are prepared to reuse old nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Sparagano_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sparagano-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Flying insects that parasitise common starlings include the <a href="/wiki/Hippoboscidae" title="Hippoboscidae">louse-fly</a> <i>Omithomya nigricornis</i><sup id="cite_ref-H1960_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1960-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Saprotrophic_nutrition" title="Saprotrophic nutrition">saprophagous</a> fly <i>Camus hemapterus</i>. The latter species breaks off the feathers of its host and lives on the fats produced by growing plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild222_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild222-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Larvae of the moth <i><a href="/wiki/Hofmannophila_pseudospretella" title="Hofmannophila pseudospretella">Hofmannophila pseudospretella</a></i> are nest scavengers, which feed on animal material such as <a href="/wiki/Faeces" class="mw-redirect" title="Faeces">faeces</a> or dead nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild251_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild251-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">Protozoan</a> blood parasites of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Haemoproteus" title="Haemoproteus">Haemoproteus</a></i> have been found in common starlings,<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild169_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild169-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> but a better known pest is the brilliant scarlet <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Gapeworm" title="Gapeworm">Syngamus trachea</a></i>. This worm moves from the lungs to the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate_trachea" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertebrate trachea">trachea</a> and may cause its host to suffocate. In Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Rook_(bird)" title="Rook (bird)">rook</a> and the common starling are the most infested wild birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild180_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild180-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Other recorded internal parasites include the spiny-headed worm <i>Prosthorhynchus transverses</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild189_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild189-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common starlings may contract <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_avium_subspecies_paratuberculosis" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycobacterium avium subspecies paratuberculosis">avian tuberculosis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-shannon_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shannon-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">avian malaria</a><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild235_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild235-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Janovy_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janovy-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Retrovirus" title="Retrovirus">retrovirus</a>-induced <a href="/wiki/Lymphoma" title="Lymphoma">lymphomas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wade_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wade-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Captive starlings often accumulate excess iron in the liver, a condition that can be prevented by adding black tea-leaves to the food.<sup id="cite_ref-crissey_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crissey-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-siebels_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-siebels-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The global population of common starlings was estimated to be 310&#160;million individuals in 2004, occupying a total area of 8,870,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3,420,000&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-BirdLifeInternational_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdLifeInternational-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Widespread throughout the Northern Hemisphere, the bird is native to <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and is found throughout Europe, northern Africa (from <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>), India (mainly in the north but regularly extending further south<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> and extending into the Maldives<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>) <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, the Middle East including <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and north-western China.<sup id="cite_ref-BirdLifeInternational_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdLifeInternational-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Resting on migration" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg/220px-Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg/330px-Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg/440px-Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_stop_on_migration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A flock resting on a pine tree during migration</div></div></div>
<p>Common starlings in the south and west of Europe and south of latitude 40°N are mainly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">resident</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-linz_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linz-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> although other populations migrate from regions where the winter is harsh, the ground frozen and food scarce. Large numbers of birds from northern Europe, Russia and Ukraine migrate south westwards or south eastwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feare54_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feare54-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In the autumn, when immigrants are arriving from eastern Europe, many of Britain's common starlings are setting off for <a href="/wiki/Iberia" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberia">Iberia</a> and North Africa. Other groups of birds are in passage across the country and the pathways of these different streams of bird may cross.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Of the 15,000 birds ringed as nestlings in <a href="/wiki/Merseyside" title="Merseyside">Merseyside</a>, England, individuals have been recovered at various times of year as far afield as Norway, Sweden, Finland, Russia, Ukraine, Poland, Germany and the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Small numbers of common starling have sporadically been observed in Japan and Hong Kong but it is unclear from where these birds originated.<sup id="cite_ref-Feare54_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feare54-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In North America, northern populations have developed a migration pattern, vacating much of Canada in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Sibley_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sibley-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Birds in the east of the country move southwards, and those from further west winter in the southwest of the US.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common starlings prefer urban or suburban areas where artificial structures and trees provide adequate nesting and <a href="/wiki/Roosting" class="mw-redirect" title="Roosting">roosting</a> sites. Reedbeds are also favoured for roosting and the birds commonly feed in grassy areas such as farmland, grazing pastures, playing fields, golf courses and airfields where short grass makes foraging easy.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> They occasionally inhabit open forests and woodlands and are sometimes found in shrubby areas such as Australian <a href="/wiki/Heathland" class="mw-redirect" title="Heathland">heathland</a>. Common starlings rarely inhabit dense, wet forests (i.e. rainforests or wet <a href="/wiki/Sclerophyll" title="Sclerophyll">sclerophyll</a> forests) but are found in coastal areas, where they nest and roost on cliffs and forage amongst <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>. Their ability to adapt to a large variety of habitats has allowed them to disperse and establish themselves in diverse locations around the world resulting in a habitat range from coastal wetlands to <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> forests, from sea cliffs to mountain ranges 1,900&#160;m (6,200&#160;ft) above sea level.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduced_populations">Introduced populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Introduced populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The common starling has been introduced to and has successfully established itself in New Zealand, Australia, South Africa, North America, <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a> and several Caribbean islands. As a result, it has also been able to migrate to <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, Southeast Asia and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
