<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Five individuals conveyed on a ship from England alighted near <a href="/wiki/Lago_de_Maracaibo" class="mw-redirect" title="Lago de Maracaibo">Lago de Maracaibo</a> in Venezuela in November 1949, but subsequently vanished.<sup id="cite_ref-long81_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-long81-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In 1987, a small population of common starlings was observed nesting in gardens in the city of <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pestnote_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pestnote-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-peris_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peris-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Since then, despite some initial attempts at eradication, the bird has been expanding its breeding range at an average rate of 7.5&#160;km (4.7&#160;mi) per year, keeping within 30&#160;km (19&#160;mi) of the Atlantic coast. In Argentina, the species makes use of a variety of natural and man-made nesting sites, particularly woodpecker holes.<sup id="cite_ref-peris_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peris-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The common starling was introduced to Australia to consume insect pests of farm crops. Early settlers looked forward to their arrival, believing that common starlings were also important for the pollination of <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>, a major agricultural product. Nest-boxes for the newly released birds were placed on farms and near crops. The common starling was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> in 1857 and Sydney two decades later.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> By the 1880s, established populations were present in the southeast of the country thanks to the work of acclimatisation committees.<sup id="cite_ref-Wooly_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wooly-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> By the 1920s, common starlings were widespread throughout <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a>, Queensland and <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>, but by then they were considered to be pests.<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Although common starlings were first sighted in <a href="/wiki/Albany,_Western_Australia" title="Albany, Western Australia">Albany, Western Australia</a> in 1917, they have been largely prevented from spreading to the state. The wide and arid <a href="/wiki/Nullarbor_Plain" title="Nullarbor Plain">Nullarbor Plain</a> provides a natural barrier and control measures have been adopted that have killed 55,000 birds over three decades.<sup id="cite_ref-dewr_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dewr-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The common starling has also colonised <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo_Island" title="Kangaroo Island">Kangaroo Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Howe_Island" title="Lord Howe Island">Lord Howe Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_Island" title="Norfolk Island">Norfolk Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-long81_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-long81-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The early settlers in New Zealand cleared the bush and found their newly planted crops were invaded by hordes of caterpillars and other insects deprived of their previous food sources. Native birds were not habituated to living in close proximity to man so the common starling was introduced from Europe along with the <a href="/wiki/House_Sparrow" class="mw-redirect" title="House Sparrow">House Sparrow</a> to control the pests. It was first brought over in 1862 by the Nelson Acclimatisation Society and other introductions followed. The birds soon became established and are now found all over the country including the subtropical <a href="/wiki/Kermadec_Islands" title="Kermadec Islands">Kermadec Islands</a> to the north and the equally distant <a href="/wiki/Macquarie_Island" title="Macquarie Island">Macquarie Island</a> far to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-olliver_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olliver-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-robertson_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robertson-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Flock of starlings" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg/220px-Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg/330px-Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg/440px-Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2863" data-file-height="1777" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_in_Napa_Valley_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flock in the <a href="/wiki/Napa_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Napa Valley">Napa Valley</a>, California</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg/220px-NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg/330px-NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg/440px-NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2167" data-file-height="1854" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NorCal2018_Common_Starling_at_Ritz-Calton_Half_Moon_Bay_S0116017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>At <a href="/wiki/Half_Moon_Bay,_California" title="Half Moon Bay, California">Half Moon Bay</a>, California</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg/220px-Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg/330px-Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg/440px-Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1157" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flying_Starling_May_2019.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A European Starling in flight, suburban St. Louis, Missouri.</div></div></div>
<p>After two failed attempts,<sup id="cite_ref-wdfw.wa.gov_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdfw.wa.gov-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> about 60 common starlings were released in 1890 into New York's <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a> by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Schieffelin" title="Eugene Schieffelin">Eugene Schieffelin</a>. He was president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Acclimatization_Society" title="American Acclimatization Society">American Acclimatization Society</a>, which reportedly tried to introduce every bird species mentioned in the works of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> into North America,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT1_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mirsky_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mirsky-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> although this has been disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> About the same date, the Portland Song Bird Club released 35 pairs of common starlings in Portland, Oregon. These birds became established but disappeared around 1902. Common starlings reappeared in the Pacific Northwest in the mid-1940s and these birds were probably descendants of the 1890 Central Park introduction.<sup id="cite_ref-wdfw.wa.gov_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdfw.wa.gov-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The original 60 birds have since swelled in number to 150&#160;million, occupying an area extending from southern Canada and Alaska to Central America.<sup id="cite_ref-Adeney_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adeney-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wdfw.wa.gov_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdfw.wa.gov-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Polynesia">Polynesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Polynesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The common starling appears to have arrived in Fiji in 1925 on Ono-i-lau and <a href="/wiki/Vatoa" title="Vatoa">Vatoa</a> islands. It may have colonised from New Zealand via <a href="/wiki/Raoul_Island" title="Raoul Island">Raoul</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kermadec_Islands" title="Kermadec Islands">Kermadec Islands</a> where it is abundant, that group being roughly equidistant between New Zealand and Fiji. Its spread in Fiji has been limited, and there are doubts about the population's viability. Tonga was colonised at about the same date and the birds there have been slowly spreading north through the group.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-watling142_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watling142-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In South Africa, the common starling was introduced in 1897 by <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>. It spread slowly, and by 1954, had reached <a href="/wiki/Clanwilliam,_Western_Cape" title="Clanwilliam, Western Cape">Clanwilliam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Port_Elizabeth" title="Port Elizabeth">Port Elizabeth</a>. It is now common in the southern Cape region, thinning out northwards to the Johannesburg area. It is present in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Cape" title="Western Cape">Western Cape</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Cape" title="Eastern Cape">Eastern Cape</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Free_State_(province)" title="Free State (province)">Free State</a> provinces of South Africa and lowland <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>, with occasional sightings in <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu-Natal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gauteng" title="Gauteng">Gauteng</a> and around the town of <a href="/wiki/Oranjemund" title="Oranjemund">Oranjemund</a> in <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>. In Southern Africa populations appear to be resident and the bird is strongly associated with man and <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic_biome" title="Anthropogenic biome">anthropogenic habitats</a>. It favours irrigated land and is absent from regions where the ground is baked so dry that it cannot probe for insects. It may compete with native birds for crevice nesting sites but the indigenous species are probably more disadvantaged by destruction of their natural habitat than they are by inter-specific competition. It breeds from September to December and outside the breeding season may congregate in large flocks, often roosting in <a href="/wiki/Reedbed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reedbed">reedbeds</a>. It is the most common bird species in urban and agricultural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-craig_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-craig-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Indies">West Indies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: West Indies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1901, the inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Kitts" title="Saint Kitts">Saint Kitts</a> petitioned the Colonial Secretary for a ″government grant of starlings to exterminate″ an outbreak of grasshoppers which was causing enormous damage to their crops.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The common starling was introduced to Jamaica in 1903, and the Bahamas and Cuba were colonised naturally from the US.<sup id="cite_ref-linz_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linz-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lever_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lever-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> This bird is fairly common but local in Jamaica, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Bahama" title="Grand Bahama">Grand Bahama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bimini" title="Bimini">Bimini</a>, and is rare in the rest of the Bahamas, eastern Cuba,<sup id="cite_ref-Raffaele_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raffaele-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Croix,_U.S._Virgin_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Croix, U.S. Virgin Islands">St. Croix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Arlott_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arlott-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The global population of the common starling is estimated to be more than 310&#160;million individuals and its numbers are not thought to be declining significantly, so the bird is classified by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> as being of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It had shown a marked increase in numbers throughout Europe from the 19th century to around the 1950s and 60s. In about 1830, <i>S. v. vulgaris</i> expanded its range in the British Isles, spreading into Ireland and areas of Scotland where it had formerly been absent, although <i>S. v. zetlandicus</i> was already present in <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> and the Outer Hebrides. The common starling has bred in northern Sweden from 1850 and in Iceland from 1935. The breeding range spread through southern France to northeastern Spain, and there were other range expansions particularly in Italy, Austria and Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpc_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpc-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It started breeding in Iberia in 1960, while the spotless starling's range had been expanding northward since the 1950s. The low rate of advance, about 4.7&#160;km (2.9&#160;mi) per year for both species, is due to the suboptimal mountain and woodland terrain. Expansion has since slowed even further due to direct competition between the two similar species where they overlap in southwestern France and northwestern Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ferrer_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrer-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Major declines in populations have been observed from 1980 onward in Sweden, Finland, northern Russia (<a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">Karelia</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic States">Baltic States</a>, and smaller declines in much of the rest of northern and central Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpc_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpc-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The bird has been adversely affected in these areas by intensive agriculture, and in several countries it has been <a href="/wiki/Red-listed" class="mw-redirect" title="Red-listed">red-listed</a> due to population declines of more than 50%. Numbers dwindled in the United Kingdom by more than 80% between 1966 and 2004; although populations in some areas such as <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> were stable or even increased, those in other areas, mainly England, declined even more sharply. The overall decline seems to be due to the low survival rate of young birds, which may be caused by changes in agricultural practices.<sup id="cite_ref-bto_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive farming</a> methods used in northern Europe mean there is less pasture and meadow habitat available, and the supply of grassland invertebrates needed for the nestlings to thrive is correspondingly reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-granbom_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-granbom-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Benefits_and_problems">Benefits and problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Benefits and problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FLi_622.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Starlings on wires" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/FLi_622.jpg/220px-FLi_622.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/FLi_622.jpg/330px-FLi_622.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/FLi_622.jpg/440px-FLi_622.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FLi_622.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Congregating on wires in France</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Starling eating fruit" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG/220px-Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG/330px-Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG/440px-Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1942" data-file-height="1322" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_no.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Feeding on a windfall apple</div></div></div>
<p>Since common starlings eat insect pests such as <a href="/wiki/Wireworm" class="mw-redirect" title="Wireworm">wireworms</a>, they are considered beneficial in northern Eurasia, and this was one of the reasons given for introducing the birds elsewhere. Around 25&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Nest_box" title="Nest box">nest boxes</a> were erected for this species in the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, and common starlings were found to be effective in controlling the grass grub <i><a href="/wiki/Costelytra_zealandica" title="Costelytra zealandica">Costelytra zealandica</a></i> in New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The original Australian introduction was facilitated by the provision of nest boxes to help this mainly <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivorous</a> bird to breed successfully,<sup id="cite_ref-H1907_39-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H1907-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and even in the US, where this is a pest species, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">Department of Agriculture</a> acknowledges that vast numbers of insects are consumed by common starlings.<sup id="cite_ref-colostate_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colostate-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common starlings introduced to areas such as Australia or North America, where other members of the genus are absent, may affect native species through competition for nest holes. In North America, <a href="/wiki/Chickadee" title="Chickadee">chickadees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuthatch" title="Nuthatch">nuthatches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">woodpeckers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purple_martin" title="Purple martin">purple martins</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallows</a> may be affected.<sup id="cite_ref-wdfw.wa.gov_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdfw.wa.gov-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fan_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fan-117">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In Australia, competitors for nesting sites include the <a href="/wiki/Crimson_rosella" title="Crimson rosella">crimson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_rosella" title="Eastern rosella">eastern rosellas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pell_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pell-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> For its role in the decline of local native species and the damages to agriculture, the common starling has been included in the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_world%27s_100_worst_invasive_species" class="mw-redirect" title="List of the world&#39;s 100 worst invasive species">List of the world's 100 worst invasive species</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>European, or common, starlings are habitat generalists meaning they are able to exploit a multitude of habitats, nest sites and food sources. This, coupled with them being lowland birds that easily coexist with humans, enables them to take advantage of other native birds, most particularly woodpecker.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> European starlings are considered aggressive <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a> that utilize an open bill probing technique (described in the “Description” section) that gives them an evolutionary advantage over birds that are <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">frugivores</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Their aggressive and gregarious behavior in terms of food thus allows them to outcompete native species. Common starlings are also aggressive in the creation of their nest cavities. Often, starlings will usurp a nest site, for example a tree hollow, and fill it rapidly with bedding and contaminants compared to other species, like the native parrots, that use little to no bedding.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> As cavity nesters, they are able to outcompete many native species in terms of habitat and nest sites. 
</p><p>Common starlings can eat and damage fruit in orchards such as <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grapes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peach" title="Peach">peaches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ribes" title="Ribes">currants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a> or dig up newly sown grain and sprouting crops.<sup id="cite_ref-pestnote_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pestnote-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-feareconf_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feareconf-123">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> They may also eat animal feed and distribute seeds through their droppings. In eastern Australia, weeds like <a href="/wiki/Asparagus_asparagoides" title="Asparagus asparagoides">bridal creeper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackberry" title="Blackberry">blackberry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrysanthemoides_monilifera" title="Chrysanthemoides monilifera">boneseed</a> are thought to have been spread by common starlings.<sup id="cite_ref-aws_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aws-124">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Agricultural damage in the US is estimated as costing about US$800&#160;million annually.<sup id="cite_ref-colostate_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colostate-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> This bird is not considered to be as damaging to agriculture in South Africa as it is in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-iziko_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iziko-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common starlings take advantage of agricultural fields, livestock facilities, and other human related sources of food and nest sites. Starlings often assault crops such as grapes, olives, and cherries by consuming excessive amounts of crops in large flock sizes and in new grain fields, starlings pull up young plants and eat the seeds.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In caged trials, it was shown that starlings eat 7-23 g of animal food daily and 20-40 g of plant food meaning a decent portion of crops are consumed by these birds.<sup id="cite_ref-bad_ref_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bad_ref-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Bird damage to grapes in 1968 cost upwards to $4.4 million while losing almost 17% of the crops.<sup id="cite_ref-bad_ref_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bad_ref-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> As well, these birds often congregate at feeding troughs to eat grain and concurrently contaminate the food and water source with their droppings that were provided for livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> For example, high protein supplements added to cattle feed are selectively eaten by common starlings.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In 1968, the cost of cattle rations consumed during winter by starlings was $84 per 1,000 starlings and is proposed to be much more expensive today given an increase in current cattle feed costs.<sup id="cite_ref-bad_ref_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bad_ref-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The English or house sparrow (<a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">Passer domesticus</a>) and the common starling are considerable agricultural pests, together causing an estimated US$ 1 billion per year in crop damages.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The large size of flocks can also cause problems. Common starlings may be sucked into aircraft jet engines, one of the worst instances of this being an incident in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> in 1960, when sixty-two people died after a <a href="/wiki/Turboprop" title="Turboprop">turboprop</a> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Air_Lines_Flight_375" title="Eastern Air Lines Flight 375">airliner</a> flew into a flock and plummeted into the sea at <a href="/wiki/Winthrop,_Massachusetts" title="Winthrop, Massachusetts">Winthrop</a> Harbor.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The large roosts of the common starling pose many safety hazards for  aircraft, mainly including the clogging of engines that concurrently shutdown the plane into descent.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> From the years 1990-2001, 852 incidents of aircraft hazard due to starlings and blackbirds were reported with 39 strikes causing major damage that cost a total of $1,607,317.<sup id="cite_ref-bad_ref_126-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bad_ref-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Starlings' droppings can contain the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Histoplasma#Histoplasma_capsulatum" title="Histoplasma">Histoplasma capsulatum</a></i>, the cause of <a href="/wiki/Histoplasmosis" title="Histoplasmosis">histoplasmosis</a> in humans. At roosting sites this fungus can thrive in accumulated droppings.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> There are a number of other <a href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a> that can potentially be transmitted by common starlings to humans,<sup id="cite_ref-colostate_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colostate-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> although the potential for the birds to spread infections may have been exaggerated.<sup id="cite_ref-wdfw.wa.gov_101-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdfw.wa.gov-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The spread of disease to livestock is also a concern, possibly more important than starling's effects on food consumption or transmission of disease to humans. The spreading of <a href="/wiki/Histoplasmosis" title="Histoplasmosis">Histoplasmosis</a> reported in a Nebraska manufacturing facility saw a loss of 10,000 pigs from the spread of the disease which is valued at $1 million US dollars loss in today's market.<sup id="cite_ref-bad_ref_126-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bad_ref-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Control">Control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of the damage they do, there have been attempts to control the numbers of both native and introduced populations of common starlings. Within the natural breeding range, this may be affected by legislation. For example, in Spain, this is a species hunted commercially as a food item, and has a closed season, whereas in France, it is classed as a pest, and the season in which it may be killed covers the greater part of the year. In Great Britain, Starlings are protected under the Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981, which makes it "illegal to intentionally kill, injure or take a starling, or to take, damage or destroy an active nest or its contents". The Wildlife Order in Northern Ireland allows, with a general licence, "an authorised person to control starlings to prevent serious damage to agriculture or preserve public health and safety".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> This species is migratory, so birds involved in control measures may have come from a wide area and breeding populations may not be greatly affected. In Europe, the varying legislation and mobile populations mean that control attempts may have limited long-term results.<sup id="cite_ref-feareconf_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feareconf-123">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Non-lethal techniques such as scaring with <a href="/wiki/Bird_scarer" title="Bird scarer">visual or auditory devices</a> have only a temporary effect in any case.<sup id="cite_ref-linz_26-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linz-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Huge urban roosts in cities can create problems due to the noise and mess made and the smell of the droppings. In 1949, so many birds landed on the clock hands of London's <a href="/wiki/Big_Ben" title="Big Ben">Big Ben</a> that it stopped, leading to unsuccessful attempts to disrupt the roosts with netting, repellent chemical on the ledges and broadcasts of common starling alarm calls. An entire episode of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Goon_Show" title="The Goon Show">The Goon Show</a></i> in 1954 was a <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">parody</a> of the futile efforts to disrupt the large common starling roosts in central London.<sup id="cite_ref-cocker_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cocker-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Starling at bird feeder" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg/170px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg/255px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg/340px-Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2745" data-file-height="3431" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-England_-bird_feeder-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Visiting a bird feeder. The adult has a black beak in the winter.</div></div></div>
<p>Where it is introduced, the common starling is unprotected by legislation, and extensive control plans may be initiated. Common starlings can be prevented from using nest boxes by ensuring that the access holes are smaller than the 1.5&#160;in (38&#160;mm) diameter they need, and the removal of perches discourages them from visiting <a href="/wiki/Bird_feeder" title="Bird feeder">bird feeders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wdfw.wa.gov_101-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdfw.wa.gov-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Western Australia banned the import of common starlings in 1895. New flocks arriving from the east are routinely shot, while the less cautious juveniles are trapped and netted.<sup id="cite_ref-Wooly_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wooly-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> New methods are being developed, such as tagging one bird and tracking it back to establish where other members of the flock roost.<sup id="cite_ref-Judas_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judas-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Another technique is to analyse the DNA of Australian common starling populations to track where the migration from eastern to western Australia is occurring so that better preventive strategies can be used.<sup id="cite_ref-Rollins_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollins-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> By 2009, only 300 common starlings were left in Western Australia, and the state committed a further A$400,000 in that year to continue the eradication programme.<sup id="cite_ref-redman_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redman-136">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, common starlings are exempt from the <a href="/wiki/Migratory_Bird_Treaty_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Migratory Bird Treaty Act">Migratory Bird Treaty Act</a>, which prohibits the taking or killing of migratory birds.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> No permit is required to remove nests and eggs or kill juveniles or adults.<sup id="cite_ref-wdfw.wa.gov_101-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdfw.wa.gov-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Research was undertaken in 1966 to identify a suitable <a href="/wiki/Avicide" title="Avicide">avicide</a> that would both kill common starlings and would readily be eaten by them. It also needed to be of low toxicity to mammals and not likely to cause the death of pets that ate dead birds. The chemical that best fitted these criteria was DRC-1339, now marketed as <a href="/wiki/Starlicide" title="Starlicide">Starlicide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-decino_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decino-138">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, the United States government poisoned, shot or trapped 1.7&#160;million birds, the largest number of any nuisance species to be culled.<sup id="cite_ref-stark_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stark-139">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, the population in the United States was estimated at 140&#160;million birds,<sup id="cite_ref-johnson_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> around 45% of the global total of 310&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The likelihood of starlings to damage the feeding operations is dependent on the number of livestock, favoring areas with more livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-Glahn_15_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glahn_15-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> They also show preference for feed types which were not whole corn but smaller feeds, creating more damage in areas where the feed was smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-Glahn_15_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glahn_15-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> They also showed feed preference based on composition.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_1777–1784_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson_1777–1784-142">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> A proposed solution to this problem is use of less palatable feed by agriculturalists, perhaps relying on larger feed types or feed which is less favorable in composition to starlings.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_1777–1784_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson_1777–1784-142">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glahn_15_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glahn_15-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> An additional solution for mitigation control involves ensuring that livestock feeding operations are not within close proximity of each other or starling roosts.<sup id="cite_ref-Glahn_15_141-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glahn_15-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Weather conditions also had an impact on whether starlings visited livestock feeding operations, with a higher likelihood to visit in colder temperatures or following snow storms.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_2011_9_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson_2011_9-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatives to managing starling populations in agricultural areas include the use of starlicide. Use of starlicide has been found to reduce the spread of <a href="/wiki/Salmonella_enterica" title="Salmonella enterica">Salmonella enterica</a> in livestock and other diseases found among livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_2011_9_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson_2011_9-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Though this does not appear to eliminate introduction of these diseases completely, it has been determined that they are contributors and starling control is a successful mitigation strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-Carlson_2011_9_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson_2011_9-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_science_and_culture">In science and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: In science and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Pet starling" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg/220px-Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg/330px-Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg/440px-Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3668" data-file-height="2615" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pet_European_Starling_Bird.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pet in a cage</div></div></div>
<p>Common starlings may be kept as pets or as laboratory animals. Austrian <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethologist</a> Konrad Lorenz wrote of them in his book <i><a href="/wiki/King_Solomon%27s_Ring_(nonfiction)" class="mw-redirect" title="King Solomon&#39;s Ring (nonfiction)">King Solomon's Ring</a></i> as "the poor man's dog" and "something to love",<sup id="cite_ref-lorentz_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lorentz-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> because nestlings are easily obtained from the wild and after careful hand rearing they are straightforward to look after.<sup id="cite_ref-lorentz_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lorentz-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kilham_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kilham-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> They adapt well to captivity, and thrive on a diet of standard bird feed and <a href="/wiki/Mealworm" title="Mealworm">mealworms</a>. Several birds may be kept in the same cage, and their inquisitiveness makes them easy to train or study. The only disadvantages are their messy and indiscriminate defecation habits and the need to take precautions against diseases that may be transmitted to humans. As a laboratory bird, the common starling is second in numbers only to the <a href="/wiki/Domestic_pigeon" title="Domestic pigeon">domestic pigeon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bvaawf_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bvaawf-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The common starling's gift for mimicry has long been recognised. In the medieval Welsh <i><a href="/wiki/Mabinogion" title="Mabinogion">Mabinogion</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Branwen" title="Branwen">Branwen</a> tamed a common starling, "taught it words", and sent it across the Irish Sea with a message to her brothers, <a href="/wiki/Bran_the_Blessed" class="mw-redirect" title="Bran the Blessed">Bran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manawydan" title="Manawydan">Manawydan</a>, who then sailed from Wales to Ireland to rescue her.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> claimed that these birds could be taught to speak whole sentences in Latin and Greek, and in <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_1" title="Henry IV, Part 1">Henry IV</a></i>, William Shakespeare had <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy_(Hotspur)" title="Henry Percy (Hotspur)">Hotspur</a> declare "The king forbade my tongue to speak of Mortimer. But I will find him when he is asleep, and in his ear I'll holler 'Mortimer!' Nay I'll have a starling shall be taught to speak nothing but Mortimer, and give it to him to keep his anger still in motion."
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MozartStarlingTune.PNG" class="image"><img alt="Mozart&#39;s &quot;starling song&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/MozartStarlingTune.PNG/300px-MozartStarlingTune.PNG" decoding="async" width="300" height="36" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/MozartStarlingTune.PNG/450px-MozartStarlingTune.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/MozartStarlingTune.PNG/600px-MozartStarlingTune.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1676" data-file-height="200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MozartStarlingTune.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mozart's "starling song"</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> had a <a href="/wiki/Mozart%27s_starling" title="Mozart&#39;s starling">pet common starling</a> which could sing part of his <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._17_(Mozart)" title="Piano Concerto No. 17 (Mozart)">Piano Concerto in G Major</a> (KV. 453).<sup id="cite_ref-cocker_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cocker-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> He had bought it from a shop after hearing it sing a phrase from a work he wrote six weeks previously, which had not yet been performed in public. He became very attached to the bird and arranged an elaborate funeral for it when it died three years later. It has been suggested that his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Musical_Joke" title="A Musical Joke">A Musical Joke</a></i> (K. 522) might be written in the comical, inconsequential style of a starling's vocalisation.<sup id="cite_ref-West_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Other people who have owned common starlings report how adept they are at picking up phrases and expressions. The words have no meaning for the starling, so they often mix them up or use them on what to humans are inappropriate occasions in their songs.<sup id="cite_ref-lorentz84_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lorentz84-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Their ability at <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicry</a> is so great that strangers have looked in vain for the human they think they have just heard speak.<sup id="cite_ref-West_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common starlings are trapped for food in some Mediterranean countries.<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The meat is tough and of low quality, so it is casseroled or made into pâté. One recipe said it should be stewed "until tender, however long that may be". Even when correctly prepared, it may still be seen as an acquired taste.<sup id="cite_ref-cocker_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cocker-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Artusi_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Artusi-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michalowski_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michalowski-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_starling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The table is based on Feare &amp; Craig (1998).<sup id="cite_ref-feare183_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feare183-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Parentheses indicate that the scientific name has changed from that originally given.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This form was described by Hodgson as <i>S. indicus</i> in <a href="/wiki/John_Edward_Gray" title="John Edward Gray">Gray</a>'s <i>Zoological Miscellany</i> of 1831, and may have <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_Priority" title="Principle of Priority">taxonomic priority</a> over <i>humii</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-gray_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gray-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ripley_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ripley-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
